    [Original] Emergency Response Safety Kit Recall: Airlife Removes Certain Broselow Pediatric Emergency Rainbow Tapes and Broselow ALS Organizers
    The affected products and recommendations for what to do with the devices below have not changed. This recall involves removing certain devices from where they are used or sold. The FDA has identified this recall as the most serious type. This device may cause serious injury or death if you continue to use it. The FDA is aware that AirLife has issued a letter to affected customers recommending certain Broselow Rainbow Tapes and Broselow ALS Organizers be removed from where they are used or sold. Affected devices: Broselow Rainbow Tape version with incorrect information is identified with the AirLife brand, 2025 Edition, and 36-23446 Rev 3 Print Version. Immediately identify, cease use, and discard affected product. On December 15, AirLife sent all affected customers a letter recommending the following actions: Check this web page for updates. The FDA is currently reviewing information about this potentially high-risk device issue and will keep the public informed as significant new information becomes available. AirLife stated that the affected product version contains three medication-related errors: The clinical consequences for incorrect medication doses based on the printed information may be serious or life-threatening. As of November 14, AirLife has not reported any serious injuries or deaths associated with this issue. The Broselow Rainbow Tape is a color-coded length-based tape used in pediatric emergencies. A childâ€™s height corresponds to a color zone and weight range. Each color zone provides pre-calculated medication doses, equipment sizes, and other emergency information to reduce the time needed for dose and equipment calculations in time-critical situations. Customers in the U.S. with adverse reactions, quality problems, or questions about this issue should contact AirLife at productquality@myairlife.com or 1-800-433-2797. The unique device identifier (UDI) helps identify individual medical devices sold in the United States from distribution to use. The UDI allows for more accurate reporting, reviewing, and analyzing of adverse event reports so that devices can be identified more quickly, and as a result, problems potentially resolved more quickly. Health care professionals and consumers may report adverse reactions or quality problems they experienced using these devices to MedWatch: The FDA Safety Information and Adverse Event Reporting Program.

    [Original] GSK, Teva quietly settle Coreg 'skinny label' dispute after long legal back-and-forth
    Feb 11, 2026 11:40am GSK Teva Pharmaceutical Coreg drug labels --> Following a more than decade-long legal back-and-forth over the generic drug carveout practice known as â€œskinnyâ€ labeling, GSK and Teva have put their dispute to bed, new court documents show. In a joint filing submitted Monday, GSKâ€”the plaintiff in the actionâ€”and Teva, in the role of defendant, told the U.S. District Court for the District of Delaware that they have â€œresolved their disputes being litigated.â€ The drugmakers provided several stipulations for their settlement, including dropping all claims, counterclaims, defenses, motions and petitions in the case, denying all pending motions as moot and keeping the Delaware federal court as the exclusive jurisdiction â€œfor the purposes of enforcing this stipulation and order.â€ The resolution of the case is more than 10 years in the making, with multiple twists and turns cropping up in recent years. The lawsuit concerns GSKâ€™s heart med Coreg, also known as carvedilol, and the skinny-label pathway for generic drugs, wherein a company can pursue a generic approval for some but not all of a branded drugâ€™s indications once it has lost certain patent protections. Teva launched a generic version of Coreg in two of the reference medâ€™s three indications back in 2007, with GSK ultimately suing the Israeli-American drugmaker in 2014 after Teva added a third indicationâ€”congestive heart failureâ€”to its genericâ€™s label at the FDAâ€™s instruction. GSKâ€™s claims largely hinged on its ownership of a method-of-use patent tied to carvedilol treatment for congestive heart failure. In the intervening years, the Delaware district court in 2017 ruled that Tevaâ€™s label encouraged doctors to prescribe its generic for heart failure despite GSKâ€™s ownership of the relevant patent, awarding GSK a $235 million verdict. That verdict was later tossed and then reinstated, and Teva eventually petitioned the Supreme Court to hear its case. In May of 2023, the High Court declined to take on the matter. At the time, a Teva spokesperson told Fierce that the company was â€œdisappointedâ€ that the Supreme Court denied its petition, while stressing that the company â€œstill has additional defenses that it will present to the Delaware District Court once the case is sent back to that court.â€ According to court documents from early 2024, Teva and GSK subsequently filed a joint request for a status conference on the case to, among other matters, set a schedule for a bench trial. While SCOTUS declined to pick up GSK and Tevaâ€™s case, the High Courtâ€™s expertise will be directed at another legal tangle on the skinny label issue, this time concerning Amarin Pharma and generic drugmaker Hikma. Last month, the Supreme Court agreed to review the case, which revolves around Hikmaâ€™s generic version of Amarinaâ€™s fish oil-derived Vascepa (icosapent ethyl). Amarinâ€™s 2020 lawsuit took issue with the fact that Hikma was allegedly able to promote its drug for Vascepaâ€™s add-on indication for cardiovascular risks, despite only winning approval in severe hypertriglyceridemia.

    [Original] Reanimal review â€“ you will never turn your back on a pelican again as long as you live
    Tarsier Studiosâ€™ *Reanimal* delivers a grim, visually striking puzzle-platformer that blends childhood fears with grotesque, surreal horror across PC, PlayStation 5, Xbox, and Switch 2. The game opens with the haunting line, â€œI thought you were dead,â€ setting a tone of dread as two child protagonists navigate a decaying world in search of lost friends. Their journey unfolds through desolate urban ruins, crumbling bulkheads, a rotting orphanage, and an impenetrable forest where dawn feels like a myth. The atmosphere is thick with decayâ€”concrete and steel creak with sorrow, and the worldâ€™s worn architecture is rendered with arresting scale and cinematographic precision. Drawing from the acclaimed *Little Nightmares* series, *Reanimal* channels the unsettling exaggeration of adult figures and bureaucratic absurdity, but diverges with moments of militarized action, such as piloting a tank or wielding a bazookaâ€”jarring contrasts to the core theme of childhood disempowerment. Gameplay combines light exploration puzzles, tense stealth, and terrifying chases, with mechanics like slow shimmying through gaps, boosting companions over ledges, and rotating mechanisms with the analog stickâ€”all executed within the first 20 minutes, a nod to the â€œcrate review systemâ€ of gaming criticism. By the six-hour runtime, the formula feels exhausted, yet the game remains hard to put down. Lovingly animated details enrich the experience: children dust themselves off after falls, beds quiver when jumped on, and small comforts like a lamp or lighter offer fleeting relief. Secret spaces, like a flooded basement where footsteps are masked by washing machines, add moments of quiet tension. The standout sequencesâ€”fleeing from or battling colossal creaturesâ€”are spectacular, particularly a harrowing encounter with a pelican that will leave players forever wary of the birds. While the game drip-feeds clues to a deeper mystery about the world and the childrenâ€™s origins, it fails to match the emotional impact of *Little Nightmares II*â€™s ending. A sequence in a flickering theater, where a projector displays macabre images, exemplifies the gameâ€™s strength in isolated, darkly beautiful momentsâ€”yet these scenes lack cohesion, leaving the narrative feeling fragmented. Despite its flaws, *Reanimal* remains a haunting, unforgettable journey through a world where even the worst nightmare is slightly more bearable with a traffic cone on your head.

    [Original] Instagram Chief Says Social Media Is Not â€˜Clinically Addictiveâ€™ in Landmark Trial
    Adam Mosseri, head of Instagram and a Meta executive since 2018, testified in a landmark federal trial on Wednesday, denying that social media is â€œclinically addictiveâ€ and defending the companyâ€™s safety measures for young users. K.G.M. settled with TikTok and Snap for undisclosed amounts before trial. Mosseri stated that Instagram implements rigorous testing of features used by teens and emphasized that while social media can cause harm, it is not clinically addictive like drugs or gambling. He compared social media use to binge-watching a TV show, noting that â€œthereâ€™s always a trade-off between safety and speech,â€ and affirmed that Instagram aims to be as safe as possible while minimizing censorship. Meta argued that K.G.M.â€™s mental health struggles stemmed from familial abuse and turmoil, not social media, citing medical records showing addiction was not a focus of her therapy. Lanier also highlighted a Meta research report indicating children with traumatic childhoods are more vulnerable to social media harm, prompting Mosseri to affirm that the company does take extra precautions for at-risk youth. He rejected the notion that safety and profitability are mutually exclusive, asserting that user safety aligns with long-term business success. A win for the plaintiff could lead to substantial damages and compel changes to app design.

    [Original] OpenAIâ€™s Biggest Challenge Is Turning Its A.I. Into a Cash Machine
    Two years after criticizing ads in ChatGPT, OpenAI CEO Sam Altman announced the company is now testing advertisements within its chatbot, marking a pivotal shift in its business strategy. Facing projected spending of $100 billion over the next four yearsâ€”despite $13 billion in revenue last yearâ€”OpenAI is under intense financial pressure to diversify income. The company aims to boost revenue by expanding into advertising and enterprise software, though both efforts are fraught with challenges. Currently, 60% of OpenAIâ€™s revenue comes from consumer products, primarily subscriptions: about 6% of the 800 million ChatGPT users pay $20 monthly for premium features. The ad push targets the free user base, but industry experts warn it risks undermining trust. OpenAI has hired Fidji Simo, a former Facebook and Instacart executive, to lead its applications division, and has recruited hundreds from Meta and X to build an ad sales team. Mark Zagorski of DoubleVerify noted OpenAI lacks the sales and tech infrastructure for ads, comparing the effort to Netflixâ€™s two-year journey to build a viable ad business. Meanwhile, OpenAI is pushing to increase enterprise revenue to 50% by year-end, offering tools like ChatGPT Enterprise and Codex, which can cost up to $200 monthly. However, competition is fierce, especially from Anthropic, whose Claude Code tool is gaining traction and which recently ran a Super Bowl ad mocking OpenAIâ€™s ad rollout: â€œAds are coming to A.I. In response, executives, including newly appointed head of science Kevin Weil, clarified that OpenAI would not take a share from individual researchers using Prism, though partnerships with large pharmaceutical companies remain possible. Altman reiterated this stance, stating OpenAI may â€œexplore partnerships where we pay the costs and share in the upside.â€ The company is also considering an IPO as early as December, though internal concerns persist over its readiness. As OpenAI navigates these complex challenges, investors and industry observers question whether it can succeed across multiple frontsâ€”advertising, enterprise, and innovationâ€”while maintaining user trust.

    [Original] Elon Musk Wants to Build an A.I. Satellite Factory on the Moon
    Elon Musk told employees at his artificial intelligence company, xAI, during an all-hands meeting on Tuesday that the company must build a lunar factory to manufacture AI satellites, using a massive â€œmass driverâ€ catapult to launch them into space. The moon-based facility, which Musk described as essential for accessing greater computing power, would serve as a foundation for xAIâ€™s AI ambitions. â€œYou have to go to the moon,â€ Musk said, adding that the resulting intelligence could be â€œincredibly exciting to see.â€ The plan builds on Muskâ€™s recent shift from Mars as the primary focus to the moon, which he now frames as a steppingstone to Mars and eventual interstellar exploration, including the search for aliens. This vision follows Muskâ€™s announcement last week to merge xAI with SpaceX, his rocket company, to advance plans for AI data centers in space. The merger coincides with SpaceXâ€™s anticipated initial public offering, potentially as early as June. While Musk did not detail how the lunar facility would be constructed, he emphasized that xAI is moving faster than any other company in AI development. â€œWeâ€™ve reached a certain scale, weâ€™re organizing the company to be more effective at this scale,â€ he said, acknowledging that some early-stage employees may not be suited for later-stage operations.     [Original] Ford Says Electric Vehicle Losses Will Continue for 3 More Years
    A few years ago, Ford Motor jumped out ahead of other established automakers by quickly rolling out three electric vehicles that it hoped would help it sell more cars and increase profits. Instead, the company is paying a high price for its early start. On Tuesday, Ford said its electric vehicle division lost $4.8 billion in 2025 and was expected to lose $4 billion to $4.5 billion in 2026. The company also said the division would continue losing money for at least two more years. â€œWe are now targeting break-even around 2029,â€ Fordâ€™s chief financial officer, Sherry House, said on Tuesday during a conference call to discuss the companyâ€™s financial results. Including the loss in 2025, Ford has lost more than $16 billion on its electric vehicle business since 2022. In December, Ford announced that it was scaling back its electric vehicle plans. It stopped making one model, the F-150 Lightning pickup truck; scrapped plans to produce an electric truck at a plant it was building in Tennessee; and canceled plans for an electric commercial van. Instead, Ford plans to make a version of the Lightning that includes a gasoline engine that can recharge the truckâ€™s batteries on the go. The company will use the Tennessee factory to make a gasoline-powered truck, and will make hybrid and gas-powered delivery vans at a location in Ohio. Ford says it still plans to make a medium-size pickup that is supposed to sell for around $30,000. That truck, expected in 2027, will use new components and production methods that Ford has said will cost a lot less. It also ended a partnership with a South Korean partner, SK On, that was supposed to jointly produce batteries. Ford has taken full ownership of a factory in Kentucky, and SK On now solely owns a plant in Tennessee. Ford took charges of $19.5 billion against its fourth-quarter earnings to cover the cost of changing its strategy. Overall, Ford lost $11.1 billion in the fourth quarter and $8.2 billion for all of 2025. The electric vehicle losses were offset by substantial profits in divisions that make internal-combustion vehicles and sell vehicles and services to commercial customers. Ford said it expected its financial performance to improve significantly in 2026. The company said this yearâ€™s adjusted earnings before interest, taxes and other items were expected to be $8 billion to $10 billion. In 2025, Ford said its adjusted earnings before interest, taxes and other items were $6.8 billion. Neal E. Boudette, a Michigan-based reporter for The Times, has been covering the auto industry for more than two decades. Share full article176Related ContentAdvertisement

    [Original] Lloyds Banking Group to close another 95 branches
    ShareSaveKevin PeacheyCost of living correspondentShareSaveGetty ImagesAnother 95 branches will be closed by Lloyds Banking Group, as many banks continue to reduce their presence on the High Street. The group said 53 Lloyds, 31 Halifax and 11 Bank of Scotland sites would shut between May this year and March 2027. Lloyds, the biggest banking group in the UK, said it offered a range of options for people to manage their money. The latest announcement is in addition an ongoing closure programme by the bank which will see 49 sites shut by October. Lloyds will have 610 branches remaining once all the announced closures are complete. A spokesperson for Lloyds said: "Customers want the freedom to bank in the way that works for them and we offer more choice and ways to manage money than ever before." More than 21 million customers use Lloyds' apps as their main way to bank, the spokesperson added. Less than two weeks ago, Santander said it was closing a further 44 branches, putting 291 jobs at risk. Banking apps and an increasing number of services provided remotely have led to less use of branches by customers, and closure programmes across the UK. However, late last year, the UK's biggest building society, the Nationwide, pledged to keep all of its 696 branches open until at least 2030, although it too has closed branches in the past. Banking hubs see banks share premises and serve customers from across the spectrum. A number of new sites have opened, but at a slower rate than the closure of individual bank branches.     [Original] Instagram boss: Too much social media use is 'personal thing'
    Adam Mosseri, CEO of Instagram, testified in a landmark California trial this week, becoming the first high-profile tech executive to appear in a case alleging social media harms minors. The six-week trial, brought by plaintiff K.G.M. and represented by attorney Mark Lanier, aims to hold tech firms accountable for mental health impacts on young users. Meta, which owns Instagram, Facebook, and WhatsApp, argues K.G.M.â€™s struggles stemmed from factors beyond Instagram use. YouTube is also named in the suit, while Snapchat and TikTok settled prior to trial. Mosseri acknowledged that Instagram should do everything possible to keep users safe, especially youth, but maintained that defining â€œexcessiveâ€ use is subjective. â€œItâ€™s important to differentiate between clinical addiction and problematic use,â€ he said, citing personal Netflix binges as examples of non-clinical overuse. He rejected the idea that a universal threshold for harmful use exists, stating one personâ€™s normal usage could be anotherâ€™s problem. During testimony, Mosseri addressed a 2019 internal email exchange involving Meta executives, including Nick Clegg, who warned that image filters altering physical appearance could harm users and risk reputational damage. While Meta initially banned filters beyond makeup mimicry, Mosseri admitted the policy was later modified, though he denied it was fully lifted. Protesters, including London resident Mariano Janin, gathered outside the courthouse, holding a photo of his daughter Mia, who died by suicide in 2021 at age 14. Janin said he traveled to Los Angeles to support efforts to restrict social media use among youth, asserting that platforms have the technology and resources to protect children. â€œIf they changed their business model it would be different,â€ he said. Metaâ€™s CEO Mark Zuckerberg and YouTube CEO Neal Mohan are expected to testify later in the trial. The case is part of a broader wave of litigation across the U.S., with thousands of suits filed by families, state attorneys general, and school districts.

    [Original] 'Fear and alienation': Senior Co-op staff complain of 'toxic' culture at the top
    Senior managers at the Co-op, the 180-year-old member-owned retailer, have raised alarms over a "toxic culture" at the executive level, citing fear, alienation, and suppression of dissent. A letter to the board, seen by the BBC and signed by multiple senior managers, alleges that leadership under CEO Shirine Khoury-Haq has fostered an environment where staff are afraid to challenge decisions, with one manager stating, â€œYou learn to look at your shoes. Nobody can speak their mind in this businessâ€”anyone who does has their card marked.â€ The letter claims leadership behaviours have significantly degraded the Co-opâ€™s culture, contributing to poor decisions, plummeting morale, and a sharp decline in profits and food waste. Despite the companyâ€™s swift response to a cyber-attack that disrupted trading for three weeks and cost Â£206m in lost sales, the letter criticizes reactive restockingâ€”such as placing parsnips on shelves meant for steakâ€”as damaging to sales and waste levels. The Co-op proceeded with a major restructuring merging retail, wholesale, and third-party buying into Group Commercial and Logistics (GCL), despite warnings from experienced staff about operational disruption and supplier confusion. Since the GCL rollout, â€œtens of colleaguesâ€ have left, including senior figures such as managing director Jerome Saint-Marc, chief commercial officer Sinead Bell, propositions director Adele Balmforth, and commercial director Rebecca Oliver-Mooney. The Co-op has seen declining monthly sales since July 2025, with market share near record lows. While the company maintains it has a solid financial position with reduced debt and strong cash reserves, critics argue the decline cannot be attributed solely to the cyber-attack. Usdaw, the shop workersâ€™ union, has echoed concerns, with national officer Jayne Allport urging staff to join the union for support. The Co-op denies the allegations, stating its culture promotes listening to colleagues and that not all views will align with final decisions. However, one source described the workplace as â€œtoxic,â€ with morale at rock bottom, while another accused leadership of â€œcorporate gaslighting.â€ The Co-opâ€™s founding principles emphasize ethical, honest, and open practices, including a commitment to a supportive work environmentâ€”values one staff member said are now betrayed. The Co-op, owned by 6.5 million members, abolished its dividend in 2014, redirecting profits to lower prices and community initiatives like academy schools. A spokesperson reiterated that colleague engagement remains high and that the company continues to foster constructive, cooperative action for long-term benefit.

    [Original] Serial rail fare evader fined Â£3,600 over 112 unpaid tickets
    Charles Brohiri, 29, has been fined Â£3,629 and given a three-month suspended prison sentence after pleading guilty to 76 charges of fare evasion on Govia Thameslink Railway (GTR) services, with a further 36 charges proven in his absence in August 2024. The court heard he travelled without a ticket on 112 journeys between London and Brighton, and on Thameslink services through Hertfordshire and Bedfordshire, over nearly two years. Despite being banned from Thameslink stations as part of his bail conditions, Brohiri continued to travel without a ticket, with 16 additional alleged offences reported since his last court appearance on 15 January. District Judge Nina Tempia described his conduct as â€œbrazenâ€ and marked by â€œself-entitlement,â€ noting his â€œdeliberate and repeatedâ€ behaviour would typically warrant a custodial sentence. However, she acknowledged the potential for rehabilitation, citing his remorse and efforts to become sober three years ago. Defence counsel Eleanor Curzon stated Brohiri had expressed regret, particularly during court appearances, and had been sleeping rough on trains, in hospitals, and libraries. She attributed his continued offending to a cycle he had fallen into, compounded by mental health challenges, lack of support, and difficulty accessing services. Brohiri, originally from Hatfield, had briefly attended university and worked as a waiter before becoming homeless. He has since committed to working with probation services to secure accommodation and employment. The court ordered him to complete 150 hours of unpaid work, attend rehabilitation, and refrain from travelling on any GTR services for one year. He will not pay prosecution costs, which totalled Â£15,120. Judge Tempia warned that any further breach would result in immediate court return. GTR spokesperson emphasized that fare evasion increases costs for passengers and diverts public funds from service improvements, though enforcement efforts and reporting tools have reduced evasion to its lowest level since 2022.

    [Original] Oatly loses long-running 'milk' battle with dairy lobby
    ShareSaveEmer MoreauBusiness reporterShareSaveGetty ImagesOatly can no longer refer to its plant-based drinks as 'oat milk'Plant-based drink maker Oatly has lost a long-running legal battle over its use of the word "milk" in its marketing. The Swedish company tried to trademark the slogan "post-milk generation" in the UK in 2021 but Dairy UK, the representative body for British dairy farmers, objected. Following rulings in several courts, the UK Supreme Court on Wednesday said Oatly could neither trademark nor use the phrase "post-milk generation". The long-running dispute has centred on Dairy UK's argument that, under trademark law, the term "milk" can only be used to refer to products that come from an animal. The Supreme Court ruled the phrase "post-milk generation" could confuse people over whether Oatly's products are completely milk-free or merely have a low milk content. Oatly's general manager for the UK and Ireland, Bryan Carroll, said the case was "a way to stifle competition and is not in the interests of the British public". "This decision creates unnecessary confusion and an uneven playing field for plant-based products that solely benefits Big Dairy." Because the relevant law only applies to food products, Oatly is still allowed to sell t-shirts bearing the slogan "post-milk generation" which it had made before the ensuing legal battle. In November 2021, Dairy UK appealed to the Intellectual Property Office (IPO) not to let the company trademark the phrase. The IPO rejected Oatly's application to trademark the phrase. The firm took the decision to the High Court, which ruled that the IPO was wrong to conclude that consumers would be confused by the phrase. But the Court of Appeal ruled in December 2024 that the term "milk" can only be used to describe animal products. Following the ruling, Judith Bryans, chief executive of Dairy UK said the body was "delighted". The ruling, she said, "helps ensure that long-established dairy terms continue to carry clear meaning for consumers". Last year, the EU parliament voted to ban the use of terms such as "oat milk" and "veggie burger" â€” but such a ban will not come into force until it is backed by the European Commission and all 27 member states. European farmers have argued such terms mislead consumers and threaten their industry. Environmentalists have said that the ban will harm sustainability efforts and is an overreach by the meat and dairy industries. Richard May, partner at law firm Osborne Clarke, said of the Supreme Court ruling: "It confirms that, even post-Brexit, the UK will continue to take a strict approach to the use of protected dairy terms, closely aligned with the EU regime. "The key principle is straightforward: if a product is not derived from animal milk, it cannot be marketed using reserved dairy designations such as 'milk' or 'cheese'." Companies like Oatly will now likely limit their use of terms like "dairy-free" to "factual" information, rather than branding and marketing, May said.

    [Original] More than 180 survivors engaged in Harrods abuse redress scheme
    ShareSaveEllie PriceNews correspondentShareSaveBBCMore than 180 survivors of abuse by the former Harrods owner Mohammed Al Fayed are currently "engaged" in its compensation scheme, the company has said. The luxury retailer said it has already paid out compensation to more than 50 women. The scheme, which was opened last March, will close to new submissions on 31 March. In October, Harrods said it had set aside more than Â£60m in its plan to compensate victims of alleged historical abuse. The compensation scheme will award each eligible claimant damages of Â£200,000, though victims who agree to be assessed by a consultant psychiatrist could receive up to Â£385,000 in compensation plus treatment costs. Survivors who are not assessed by a psychiatrist will receive lower payments. Applications submitted by 31 March will continue to be processed after that deadline. The department store also said it will continue offering counselling support after the deadline, including from its so-called Independent Survivor Advocate. Human rights campaigner Dame Jasvinder Sanghera was appointed by Harrods for the Advocate role in 2024, promising to meet as many survivors as possible and guide them through the retailer's compensation process. Justice for Harrods Survivors, which represents some victims, said only a "truly independent" process could address the needs of survivors. Multiple women have accused Al Fayed, who owned the luxury store between 1985 to 2010, of rape and sexual assault. The Met Police said that 146 people have come forward to report a crime in their investigation into Al Fayed. As well as its flagship store in Knightsbridge, London, the Harrods Group operates smaller stores at London's Heathrow and Gatwick airports, as well as a small chain of beauty stores. KP Law, who represents more than 260 survivors, has been critical of the Redress Scheme citing concerns Harrods retain "complete control" of the scheme. Tom Fletcher, a specialist lawyer at Irwin Mitchell, which is supporting some victims, said it is pleased to have secured settlements for some of the firm's clients. He said: "Whilst no amount of money will ever be enough, the amounts of compensation recovered under the scheme recognises the significance of the abuse our clients went through while also allowing them funds to access the specialist support they require to try and move forward with their lives the best they can."

    [Original] Children bombarded with weight loss drug ads online, says commissioner

The report highlighted that children also encounter unregulated beauty content, including promotions for cosmetic procedures like lip fillers, which are deemed â€œunavoidableâ€ online. Dr Peter Macaulay, a senior lecturer in Psychology at the University of Derby, supported ending child-targeted advertising, emphasizing the need for stronger platform accountability, enforcement of age-appropriate design, and digital literacy education. Dame Rachel proposed amending the Online Safety Act (OSA) to impose a â€œclear duty of careâ€ on social media platforms to prevent advertising to children. The debate continues over how best to safeguard young users from harmful content and exploitative advertising.

    [Original] Disney advert banned for showing 'disturbing' severed body
    A Disney advertisement for the upcoming sci-fi film *Predator: Badlands* has been banned by the UKâ€™s Advertising Standards Authority (ASA) for being likely to frighten and distress children. The digital poster, displayed on a roadside in Giffnock, Glasgow, featured a large alien creature holding aloft the severed body of a smaller, human-like figure, with its spine exposed and lower torso missing. The ad included close-up shots of the alienâ€™s distorted face and sharp fangs, accompanied by the caption â€œwelcome to a world of hurt.â€ Two parents complained to the ASA, calling the imagery â€œinappropriate and disturbingâ€ for young children. Disneyâ€™s subsidiary, Twentieth Century Studios, defended the ad, stating the severed figure was a robot, or â€œsynth,â€ and that its dismemberment emphasized its non-human nature. The company noted the film is rated 12A and that the ad was designed accordingly. It also highlighted that the scene appeared for less than two seconds in a 10-second advert and that prior versions had undergone third-party â€œsense checkingâ€ and been revised based on feedback. Despite these arguments, the ASA ruled the ad breached the UK advertising codeâ€™s provisions on social responsibility and harm or offence. The watchdog acknowledged Disneyâ€™s claim that the figure was a robot but stated this was not clear from the adâ€™s visuals. The exposed spine, severed torso, and menacing portrayal of the alien were deemed â€œdisturbingâ€ and likely to cause fear in children. The close-up shots of the alienâ€™s face and the â€œwelcome to a world of hurtâ€ caption were also considered likely to frighten younger viewers. The ASA concluded the ad was unsuitable for a general audience, particularly children, and ordered it to be withdrawn from circulation in its current form. It also reminded Disney of its duty to ensure future advertisements do not expose children to distressing content. A Disney spokesperson said the company acknowledged the ruling, emphasizing its commitment to audience responsibility and adherence to advertising standards.

    [Original] Ford says it took an extra $900m tariff hit last year
    ShareSaveDanielle KayeBusiness reporterShareSaveReutersFord executives said the US carmaker's tariff costs were $900mn (Â£660mn) higher than expected last year.Ford said the US car maker's tariff costs were $900m (Â£660m) higher than expected last year because of a last minute change to the Trump administration's tariff relief program. The scheme is meant to help car firms offset US President Donald Trump's levies, and lets car makers that import parts for vehicles assembled in the US apply for credits. But administration officials told the company in December of a new, later effective date for the policy, leading to fewer gains from the credits than anticipated. Chief executive Jim Farley said Ford spent double what it had expected on tariffs in 2025 - roughly $2bn - due to "the unexpected and late year change in tariff credits for auto parts". Ford's higher-than-expected tariff bill underscores the volatility automakers continue to face as they grapple with tariff costs and lobby for exemptions from the levies. Separately, Ford had previously disclosed a $19.5bn hit as a result of its shift away from electric vehicle plans. Those charges also contributed to its fourth-quarter net loss of $11.1bn. The vehicle manufacturer had said it was backing away from plans to make large EVs, citing lacklustre demand and recent regulatory changes under Trump. The business case for leaning heavily into EV production, specifically large-sized EV models, has "eroded", the company had said. Ford is instead investing in producing profitable hybrid and gas-powered vehicles and smaller, more affordable EV models. The carmaker's decision to alter its EV plans followed a similar announcement from General Motors in October. General Motors said it would take a $1.6bn hit as it rolls back its EV ambitions amid weakening demand. A fire at an aluminium supplier also weighed on Ford's profits last year. Still, despite the tariff bill and net loss, the company reported quarterly revenue that beat analysts' expectations. Executives also said they are forecasting an uptick in profit this year, and predicted a reduction in losses in its EV business. The carmaker's shares rose slightly in after-hours trading in the US.

    [Original] U.S. Health Officials Defend Rejection of Modernaâ€™s Flu Vaccine
    The Food and Drug Administration (FDA) reversed its earlier decision to reject Modernaâ€™s application for approval of its mRNA flu vaccine, following public scrutiny and internal debate. The initial rejection, made by Dr. Vinay Prasad, the top vaccine regulator, overrode staff recommendations and halted the review process despite late-stage trials demonstrating the vaccineâ€™s safety and efficacy in adults 50 and older. Dr. Marty Makary, FDA commissioner, acknowledged the decision was part of ongoing dialogue with drugmakers and suggested approval remains possible, stating the agency would continue working with Moderna. The FDA defended its stance, asserting that Moderna failed to use a high-dose flu vaccineâ€”specifically GSKâ€™s Fluarixâ€”as a control in trials involving adults 65 and older, exposing participants to increased risk of severe illness. Andrew Nixon, a Department of Health and Human Services spokesperson, emphasized this deviation from recommended trial design. However, Moderna disputed the claim, citing multiple meetings with the FDA beginning in April 2024 in which officials approved the use of Fluarix as the comparator. Chris Ridley, a company spokesperson, stated the trial design was FDA-approved and that communications were consistent with continuing the study. Internal FDA divisions emerged, with Dr. Prasad aligning with some junior scientists but clashing with Dr. David Kaslow, director of the Office of Vaccines Research and Review. Makary cited ethical concerns over the control group, noting some critics argued the trial was unethical for not offering the best available option to seniors. The controversy comes amid broader tensions under Health Secretary Robert F. Kennedy Jr., a vocal critic of mRNA vaccines, who has pushed for placebo-controlled trialsâ€”a position widely rejected by vaccine experts. Kennedy has not been involved in the Moderna flu vaccine review, according to a senior FDA official. Since Kennedyâ€™s appointment, Moderna has faced setbacks, including the cancellation of a nearly $600 million bird flu vaccine contract and a $500 million reduction in funding for mRNA projects. The FDA also tightened criteria for Covid vaccine eligibility. Modernaâ€™s $750 million investment by Blackstone funded a 41,000-person clinical trial, which confirmed the mRNA flu vaccineâ€™s safety and effectiveness. The technology could enable rapid response to emerging flu strains and allow for country-specific vaccine customization. Dr. Prasadâ€™s rejection was unusual, as the FDA rarely declines to review applications. Prasad, known for criticizing the agencyâ€™s past leniency, held a prominent academic role before joining the FDA.

    [Original] Powerful cyclone kills at least 31 as it tears through Madagascar port
    ShareSaveJean OtalorShareSaveWatch: An overturned truck lies on its side, as drone footage shows damage caused by deadly cycloneAt least 31 people have died after a powerful cyclone struck Madagascar, says the disaster authority in the Indian Ocean island. Cyclone Gezani made landfall on Tuesday, hitting the island's main port, Toamasina. Madagascar's disaster management office said there was "total chaos" - reporting that houses collapsed in the impact zone, where the bodies were found. Neighbourhoods were plunged into darkness as power lines snapped, while trees were uprooted and roofs ripped off. "What happened is a disaster, nearly 75% of the city of Toamasina was destroyed," the country's military leader Colonel Michael Randrianirina, who seized power in October, told the AFP news agency. "The current situation exceeds Madagascar's capabilities alone," he added. The cyclone's landfall is likely to have been one of the most intense recorded around the city in the satellite era, according to the CMRS cyclone forecaster on France's Reunion island, AFP reports. The National Office for Risk and Disaster Management said many were killed when houses collapsed. Cyclone Gezani hit Toamasina - the country's second-largest city - with winds reaching 250 km/hour (155 mph). "It's total chaos, 90% of house roofs have been blown off, entirely or in part," the head of disaster management at the Action Against Hunger aid agency, Rija Randrianarisoa, told AFP. Madagascar's disaster management office has evacuated dozens of injured people and hundreds of residents from a district around Toamasina, home to 400,000 people. Residents in and around Toamasina described scenes of chaos as the cyclone made landfall. "I have never experienced winds this violent... The doors and windows are made of metal, but they are being violently shaken," Harimanga Ranaivo told the Reuters news agency. Gezani is the second cyclone to hit Madagascar this year. It comes 10 days after tropical cyclone Fytia killed 14 and displaced over 31,000 people, according to the UN's humanitarian office. Ahead of the cyclone's arrival, officials shuttered schools and rushed to prepare emergency shelters. Madagascar's meteorological service said on Wednesday morning that Gezani had weakened to a moderate tropical storm and had moved westward inland, about 100km (60 miles) north of the capital, Antananarivo. "Gezani will cross the central highlands from east to west today, before moving out to sea into the Mozambique Channel this evening or tonight," the service said. Cyclone season in the Indian Ocean around Madagascar normally lasts from November to April and sees around a dozen storms each year, AFP reports. Go to BBCAfrica.com for more news from the African continent. Follow us on Twitter @BBCAfrica, on Facebook at BBC Africa or on Instagram at bbcafrica

    [Original] Russian strike kills 3 Ukrainian toddlers and father, with pregnant mother injured
    ShareSaveClaire KeenanandJaroslav LukivShareSaveKharkiv regional military administrationA soft toy is seen among the wreckage of a house in the north-eastern Ukrainian town of Bohodukhiv after a Russian drone strikeAn overnight Russian drone strike has killed three toddlers and their father and also injured their pregnant mother in Ukraine's north-eastern Kharkiv region, local officials say. Regional head Oleh Synegubov said Ivan and Vladyslav, two-year-old twin boys, and their one-year-old sister Myroslava died when their house was hit in the town of Bohodukhiv. Their father was named as Hryhoriy, aged 34. The toddlers' mother Olha, 35, had minor burns - but survived. Russia has renewed its almost daily drone and missile strikes after a week-long pause earlier this month that Donald Trump had asked Vladimir Putin to observe as a fierce cold swept Ukraine. In a statement on social media, Synegubov described the Russian attack on Bohodukhiv as "brutal". He said that the family that was in the hit house had recently evacuated from a town near the Russian border, trying to escape constant shelling. "It was their first night in the new place," the regional head added. Bohodukhiv, north-west of Ukraine's second largest city of Kharkiv, has been under almost daily Russian attack in recent days. On Monday, a 41-year-old woman and a 10-year-old boy were killed in the town, local officials said. Russian forces also dropped seven bombs on the city of Slovyansk on Tuesday, Donetsk regional head Vadym Filashkin said on Telegram. Three people, including an 11-year-old girl, were killed. Moscow has increased pressure on the area by targeting transport and energy infrastructure with a record number of ballistic missiles, Ukrainian President Volodymyr Zelensky has said. A state of emergency has been declared over the energy situation in the Kharkiv region by local authorities. It comes after a so-called "energy truce", which was meant to allow Ukraine's energy sector a reprieve during -20C (-4F) temperatures, expired last week. Tens of thousands of people across Ukraine are experiencing severe power cuts, and many households have no running water or heating. Zelensky has said the US wants the war with Russia to end by June, and that both sides had been invited to the US for talks next week. Meanwhile, a massive attack by Ukrainian drones on Volzhsky, in the Volgograd region in Russia, was reported on Wednesday night. Regional Governor Andrei Bocharov said an apartment was damaged in the strike and a fire broke out at a factory. Russia launched a full-scale invasion of Ukraine in February 2022.

    [Original] Ghanaians embrace 'Fugu Day' after online mockery of traditional outfits
    Ghanaians have embraced â€œFugu Dayâ€ on Wednesdays, a national initiative encouraging the wearing of the traditional northern Ghanaian garment, the fugu, after President John Dramani Mahama wore it during a state visit to Zambia. The move followed online mockery from Zambians who dubbed the outfit a â€œblouse,â€ sparking a national pride response. Tourism Minister Abla Dzifa Gomashie said the weekly display would project Ghanaâ€™s identity globally, promote cultural confidence, and stimulate the creative economy, benefiting weavers, designers, and traders. The fugu, also known as batakari, is a hand-woven, poncho-style top made from thick cotton strips, traditionally worn by men with matching trousers and hats, though women also wear it in longer or dress forms. It symbolizes royalty and authority and was worn by Ghanaâ€™s founding father, Kwame Nkrumah, and five others during the 1957 independence celebrations. Workers at the National Health Insurance Scheme (NHIS) and civil servants like Raymond Avenor in Accra praised the fugu for its comfort, ease of wear, and cultural significance, with Avenor noting it requires no ironing and reflects northern heritage. Andrews Tetteh Zutah, who owns over seven fugus, expressed a desire to wear the garment daily despite office constraints. Demand has surged, with fugu maker Elijah Sulemana Musah reporting increased orders from Ghanaian celebrities like Okyeame Kwame and Kwabena Kwabena, as well as interest from Zambians. The trend has gone viral on social media, with Ghanaians promoting â€œFugu Fridayâ€ and Zambians showcasing their traditional siziba, prompting Zambian President Hakainde Hichilema to announce plans to order fugus from Ghana. Mahama also suggested bulk exports to Zambia and highlighted the garmentâ€™s historical importance, referencing Nkrumahâ€™s 1957 use and his own recent UN appearance in a fugu. Past leaders including Nana Akufo-Addo, John Kufuor, and Jerry John Rawlings have also publicly worn the outfit, underscoring its enduring cultural and political symbolism.

Chen had served as class discipline monitor, while Yu, known for being mischievous, had often been disciplined by himâ€”and even received math homework tutoring from Chen.     [Original] å…¨å›½æˆ¿ä»·æ­¢è·Œä¿¡å·åˆç°
    In May 2024, the national real estate market showed signs of stabilizing after a downturn. According to data from the National Bureau of Statistics, the number of cities among 70 major and medium-sized cities experiencing month-on-month declines in new home prices decreased by 12 compared to the previous month. In first-tier cities, new home prices in Beijing, Shanghai, and Shenzhen saw slight month-on-month increases; second-tier cities such as Chengdu, Hangzhou, and Nanjing showed signs of stabilization and recovery. The secondary housing market rebounded more noticeably, with transaction volumes increasing by over 20% month-on-month in multiple regions, and some cities experiencing "rising prices and volumes." This round of recovery has been driven by a series of policy measures introduced by both central and local governments to stabilize the property market. Since the end of 2023, the People's Bank of China has repeatedly cut reserve requirement ratios and interest rates, bringing mortgage rates to historically low levels. Many cities have lifted purchase restrictions and sales restrictions, while some have introduced "trade-in" programs and home purchase subsidies. In the first quarter of 2024, the central government explicitly reaffirmed support for both primary and improved housing demand through "city-specific policies," prompting cities such as Nanjing, Suzhou, and Hefei to successively relax home purchase qualifications, reduce down payment ratios, and increase housing fund loan limits. Market analysts point out that halting the decline in home prices helps stabilize market expectations, alleviates financial pressure on real estate developers, boosts industry confidence, and may stimulate recovery in upstream and downstream industries such as building materials, home furnishings, and home appliances. However, experts caution that the market remains in an early stage of recovery, and a full rebound will take time, with pronounced regional disparities; the pressure to clear inventory remains significant in third- and fourth-tier cities. Future policies must maintain continuity and precision, avoid excessive liquidity, and promote a steady and healthy development of the real estate market.
